<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Millennials</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Generation Y" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Generation_Y_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Generation Y (disambiguation)">Generation Y (disambiguation)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Millennials_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Millennials (disambiguation)">Millennials (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><td class="navbox-abovebelow" style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of a series on</td></tr><tr><th class="navbox-abovebelow" style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Generation" title="Generation">Generations</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lost_Generation" title="Lost Generation">Lost Generation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/G.I._Generation" title="G.I. Generation">G.I. Generation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Silent_Generation" title="Silent Generation">Silent Generation</a></li>

<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Millennials</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Generation_Z" title="Generation Z">Generation Z</a></li></ul></td>
</tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Millennials</b>, also known as <b>Generation Y</b> or <b>Gen Y</b>, are the <a href="/wiki/Generation" title="Generation">generational</a> <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demographic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cohort_(statistics)" title="Cohort (statistics)">cohort</a> following <a href="/wiki/Generation_X" title="Generation X">Generation X</a> and preceding <a href="/wiki/Generation_Z" title="Generation Z">Generation Z</a>. There are no precise dates for when this cohort starts or ends; demographers and researchers typically use the early 1980s as starting birth years and the mid-1990s to early 2000s as ending birth years.  Millennials are sometimes referred to as "echo boomers" due to a major surge in birth rates in the 1980s and 1990s, and because millennials are often the children of the <a href="/wiki/Baby_boomers" title="Baby boomers">baby boomers</a>. The 20th-century trend toward smaller families in developed countries continued, however, so the relative impact of the "baby boom echo" was generally less pronounced than the <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93World_War_II_baby_boom" class="mw-redirect" title="Post–World War II baby boom">post–World War II baby boom</a>.
</p><p>Although Millennial characteristics vary by region, depending on social and economic conditions, the <a href="/wiki/Generation" title="Generation">generation</a> is generally marked by an increased use and familiarity with communications, media, and digital technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-Fries_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fries-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> has had a major impact on this generation because it has caused historically high levels of unemployment among young people, and has led to speculation about possible long-term economic and social damage to this generation.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<p>Authors <a href="/wiki/William_Strauss" title="William Strauss">William Strauss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neil_Howe" title="Neil Howe">Neil Howe</a> are widely credited with naming the millennials.<sup id="cite_ref-Horovitz_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horovitz-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> They coined the term in 1987, around the time children born in 1982 were entering preschool, and the media were first identifying their prospective link to the new <a href="/wiki/Millennium" title="Millennium">millennium</a> as the high school graduating class of 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss_2000_370_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss_2000_370-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> They wrote about the cohort in their books <i>Generations: The History of America's Future, 1584 to 2069</i> (1991)<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Millennials Rising: The Next Great Generation</i> (2000).<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss_2000_370_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss_2000_370-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1993, an <i><a href="/wiki/Advertising_Age" title="Advertising Age">Advertising Age</a></i> editorial coined the phrase <i>Generation Y</i> to describe those who were aged 11 or younger as well as the <a href="/wiki/Teenager" class="mw-redirect" title="Teenager">teenagers</a> of the upcoming ten years who were defined as different from <a href="/wiki/Generation_X" title="Generation X">Generation X</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> According to journalist Bruce Horovitz, in 2012, <i>Ad Age</i> "threw in the towel by conceding that millennials is a better name than Gen Y",<sup id="cite_ref-Horovitz_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horovitz-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and by 2014, a past director of data strategy at <i>Ad Age</i> said to NPR "the Generation Y label was a placeholder until we found out more about them".<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Millennials are sometimes called <i>Echo Boomers</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-usa110605_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usa110605-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> due to their being the offspring of the baby boomers and due to the significant increase in birth rates from the early 1980s to mid 1990s, mirroring that of their parents. In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Birth_rates" class="mw-redirect" title="Birth rates">birth rates</a> peaked in August 1990<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Font_size_Print_E-mail_Share_Page_1_of_2_By_Rebecca_Leung_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Font_size_Print_E-mail_Share_Page_1_of_2_By_Rebecca_Leung-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and a 20th-century trend toward smaller families in developed countries continued.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In his book <i>The Lucky Few: Between the <a href="/wiki/Greatest_Generation" class="mw-redirect" title="Greatest Generation">Greatest Generation</a> and the Baby Boom</i>, author Elwood Carlson called this cohort the "New Boomers".<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Psychologist" title="Psychologist">Psychologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Jean_Twenge" title="Jean Twenge">Jean Twenge</a> described millennials as "Generation Me" in her 2006 book <i>Generation Me: Why Today’s Young Americans Are More Confident, Assertive, Entitled—and More Miserable Than Ever Before</i>, which was updated in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine ran a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cover_story" class="extiw" title="wikt:cover story">cover story</a> titled <i>Millennials: The Me Me Me Generation</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> used the term <i>Generation 9/11</i> to refer to young people who were between the ages of 10 and 20 years during the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">terrorist acts of 11 September 2001</a>. The first reference to "Generation 9/11" was made in the cover story of the 12 November 2001 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Newsweek_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsweek-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Alternative names for this group proposed include <i>Generation We</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <i>Global Generation</i>, <i>Generation Next</i><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and the <i>Net Generation</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shapira_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shapira-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chinese millennials are commonly called the 1980s and 1990s <a href="/wiki/Generation" title="Generation">generations</a>. At a 2015 conference in Shanghai organized by <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">University of Southern California</a>'s US-China Institute, millennials in China were examined and contrasted with American millennials.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Findings included millennials' marriage, childbearing, and child raising preferences, life and career ambitions, and attitudes towards volunteerism and activism.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_and_age_range_definitions">Date and age range definitions</span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this section is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Millennials##" title="Talk:Millennials">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span>  <small><i>(July 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A minority of demographers and researchers start the generation in the mid-to-late 1970s, such as <a href="/wiki/MetLife" title="MetLife">MetLife</a> which uses birth dates ranging 1977–1994,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_ratings" title="Nielsen ratings">Nielsen Media Research</a> which uses the earliest dates from 1977 and the latest dates 1995 or 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of researchers and demographers start the generation in the early 1980s, with some ending the generation in the mid-1990s. <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>'s McCrindle Research<sup id="cite_ref-McCrindle_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCrindle-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> uses 1980–1994 as Generation Y birth years. A 2013 <a href="/wiki/PricewaterhouseCoopers" title="PricewaterhouseCoopers">PricewaterhouseCoopers</a><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> report used 1980 to 1995. <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup Inc.</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dale_Carnegie" title="Dale Carnegie">MSW Research</a><sup id="cite_ref-alecarnegie_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alecarnegie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> use 1980–1996.  <a href="/wiki/Ernst_%26_Young" title="Ernst &amp; Young">Ernst and Young</a> uses 1981–1996.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>A 2018 report from <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> defines Millennials as born from 1981 to 1996, choosing these dates for "key political, economic and social factors", including <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11th terrorist attacks</a>. This range makes Millennials 5 to 20 years old at the time of the attacks so "old enough to comprehend the historical significance." Pew indicated they'd use 1981 to 1996 for future publications but would remain open to date recalibration.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2018_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2018-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some end the generation in the late 1990s or early 2000s. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> defines the millennial generation as those born from 1982–2000.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Resolution_Foundation" title="Resolution Foundation">Resolution Foundation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> all use 1980–2000.  SYZYGY, a digital service agency partially owned by <a href="/wiki/WPP_plc" title="WPP plc">WPP</a>, uses 1981–1998,<sup id="cite_ref-prnews_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prnews-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-syzygy_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-syzygy-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>. The Asia Business Unit of Corporate Directions, Inc describes Millennials as born between 1981-2000,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="United States Chamber of Commerce">United States Chamber of Commerce</a> uses 1980-1999<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>. The <a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster" title="Merriam-Webster">Merriam-Webster</a> Online Dictionary describes Millennials as those born roughly between the 1980s and 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2013 <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine cover story used 1980 or 1981 as start dates.<sup id="cite_ref-TimeMagazine_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimeMagazine-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Demographers <a href="/wiki/Strauss%E2%80%93Howe_generational_theory" title="Strauss–Howe generational theory">William Straus and Neil Howe</a> who are widely credited with coining the term, define Millennials as born between 1982–2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Horovitz_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horovitz-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> However, Howe described the dividing line between millennials and the following <a href="/wiki/Generation_Z" title="Generation Z">Generation Z</a> as "tentative", saying "you can’t be sure where history will someday draw a cohort dividing line until a generation fully comes of age". He noted that the millennials' range beginning in 1982 would point to the next generation's window starting between 2000 and 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In his 2008 book <i>The Lucky Few: Between the Greatest Generation and the Baby Boom</i>, author Elwood Carlson defined this cohort as born between 1983–2001, based on the upswing in births after 1983 and finishing with the "political and social challenges" that occurred after the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 terrorist acts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, <a href="/wiki/Public_Interest_Research_Group" title="Public Interest Research Group">U.S PIRG</a> described millennials as those born between 1983 and 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> On the American television program <i><a href="/wiki/Survivor_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Survivor (U.S. TV series)">Survivor</a></i>, for their 33rd season, subtitled <i><a href="/wiki/Survivor:_Millennials_vs._Gen_X" title="Survivor: Millennials vs. Gen X">Millennials vs. Gen X</a></i>, the "Millennial tribe" consisted of individuals born between 1984 and 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to birth-year overlap between definitions of Generation X and Millennials, some individuals born in the late 1970s and early 1980s see themselves as being "between" the two generations.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Huffington1_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huffington1-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Names given to those born in the Generation X and Millennial <a href="/wiki/Cusper" title="Cusper">cusp</a> years include <a href="/wiki/Xennials" title="Xennials">Xennials</a>, Generation Catalano, and the Oregon Trail Generation.<sup id="cite_ref-Huffington1_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huffington1-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traits">Traits</span></h2>
<p>Authors <a href="/wiki/Strauss_and_Howe" class="mw-redirect" title="Strauss and Howe">William Strauss and Neil Howe</a> believe that each generation has common characteristics that give it a specific character with four basic generational archetypes, repeating in a cycle. According to their hypothesis, they predicted millennials will become more like the "civic-minded" <a href="/wiki/G.I._Generation" title="G.I. Generation">G.I. Generation</a> with a strong sense of community both local and global.<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss_2000_370_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss_2000_370-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Strauss and Howe ascribe seven basic traits to the Millennial cohort: special, sheltered, confident, team-oriented, conventional, pressured, and achieving. Arthur E. Levine, author of <i>When Hope and Fear Collide: A Portrait of Today's College Student</i> describes these generational images as "stereotypes".<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Strauss and Howe's research has been influential, but it also has critics.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Psychologist Jean Twenge says Strauss and Howe's assertions are overly-deterministic, non-falsifiable, and unsupported by rigorous evidence. Twenge, the author of the 2006 book <i>Generation Me</i>, considers millennials, along with younger members of Generation X, to be part of what she calls "Generation Me".<sup id="cite_ref-auto_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Twenge attributes millennials with the traits of confidence and tolerance, but also describes a sense of <a href="/wiki/Entitlement" title="Entitlement">entitlement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Narcissism" title="Narcissism">narcissism</a>, based on personality surveys showing increased narcissism among millennials compared to preceding generations when they were teens and in their twenties. She questions the predictions of Strauss and Howe that this generation will turn out civic-minded.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto2_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> A 2016 study by <i>SYZYGY</i> a digital service agency, found millennials in the U.S. continue to exhibit elevated scores on the <a href="/wiki/Narcissistic_Personality_Inventory" title="Narcissistic Personality Inventory">Narcissistic Personality Inventory</a> as they age, finding millennials exhibited 16% more narcissism than older adults, with males scoring higher on average than females. The study examined two types of narcissism: grandiose narcissism, described as "the narcissism of <a href="/wiki/Extraversion" class="mw-redirect" title="Extraversion">extraverts</a>, characterized by attention-seeking behavior, power and dominance", and vulnerable narcissism, described as "the narcissism of <a href="/wiki/Introversion" class="mw-redirect" title="Introversion">introverts</a>, characterized by an acute sense of self-entitlement and defensiveness."<sup id="cite_ref-prnews_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prnews-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-syzygy_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-syzygy-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>'s "Monitoring the Future" study of high school seniors (conducted continually since 1975) and the American Freshman survey, conducted by UCLA's Higher Education Research Institute of new college students since 1966, showed an increase in the proportion of students who consider wealth a very important attribute, from 45% for Baby Boomers (surveyed between 1967 and 1985) to 70% for Gen Xers, and 75% for millennials. The percentage who said it was important to keep abreast of political affairs fell, from 50% for Baby Boomers to 39% for Gen Xers, and 35% for millennials. The notion of "developing a meaningful philosophy of life" decreased the most across generations, from 73% for Boomers to 45% for millennials. The willingness to be involved in an environmental cleanup program dropped from 33% for Baby Boomers to 21% for millennials.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Millennials show a willingness to vote more than previous generations. With voter rates being just below 50% for the last four presidential cycles, they have already surpassed Gen Xers of the same age who were at just 36%.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2013 Pew Research Poll found that 84% of millennials, born since 1980, who were at that time between the ages of 18 and 32, favored <a href="/wiki/Legalization_of_marijuana" class="mw-redirect" title="Legalization of marijuana">legalizing the use of marijuana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto9_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the Pew Research Center also conducted research regarding generational identity that said a majority did not like the "Millennial" label.<sup id="cite_ref-pporg_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pporg-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2014, the Pew Research Center issued a report about how "millennials in adulthood" are "detached from institutions and networked with friends."<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Millennials_in_Adulthood_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millennials_in_Adulthood-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The report said millennials are somewhat more upbeat than older adults about America's future, with 49% of millennials saying the country’s best years are ahead though they're the first in the modern era to have higher levels of student loan debt and unemployment.
</p><p>Fred Bonner, a Samuel DeWitt Proctor Chair in Education at <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a> and author of <i>Diverse Millennial Students in College: Implications for Faculty and Student Affairs</i>, believes that much of the commentary on the Millennial Generation may be partially accurate, but overly general and that many of the traits they describe apply primarily to "white, affluent teenagers who accomplish great things as they grow up in the suburbs, who confront anxiety when applying to super-selective colleges, and who multitask with ease as their <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_parents" class="mw-redirect" title="Helicopter parents">helicopter parents</a> hover reassuringly above them." During class discussions, Bonner listened to black and Hispanic students describe how some or all of the so-called core traits did not apply to them. They often said that the "special" trait, in particular, is unrecognizable. Other socio-economic groups often do not display the same attributes commonly attributed to millennials. "It's not that many diverse parents don't want to treat their kids as special," he says, "but they often don't have the <a href="/wiki/Social_capital" title="Social capital">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_capital" title="Cultural capital">cultural capital</a>, the time and resources, to do that."<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his book <i>Fast Future</i>, author David Burstein describes millennials' approach to social change as "pragmatic idealism" with a deep desire to make the world a better place, combined with an understanding that doing so requires building new institutions while working inside and outside existing institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plenari_09-06-2015_(18482335830).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Plenari_09-06-2015_%2818482335830%29.jpg/220px-Plenari_09-06-2015_%2818482335830%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Plenari_09-06-2015_%2818482335830%29.jpg/330px-Plenari_09-06-2015_%2818482335830%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Plenari_09-06-2015_%2818482335830%29.jpg/440px-Plenari_09-06-2015_%2818482335830%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plenari_09-06-2015_(18482335830).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extensive use of Internet and mobile technology starting at a young age is a defining trait of millennials</div></div></div>
<p>Elza Venter, an educational psychologist and lecturer at Unisa, South Africa, in the Department of Psychology of Education, believes members of Generation Y are digital natives because they have grown up experiencing digital technology and have known it all their lives. Prensky <sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> coined the concept ‘digital natives’ because this generation are ‘native speakers of the digital language of computers, video games and the internet’. This generation spans 20 years and its older members use a combination of face-to-face communication and computer <a href="/wiki/Mediated_communication" title="Mediated communication">mediated communication</a>, while its younger members use mainly electronic and digital technologies for interpersonal communication.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Workplace_attitudes">Workplace attitudes</span></h2>
<p>There are vast, and conflicting, amounts of literature and empirical studies discussing the existence of generational differences as it pertains to the workplace.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The majority of research concludes millennials differ from both their generational cohort predecessors, and can be characterized by a preference for a flat corporate culture, an emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Work%E2%80%93life_balance" title="Work–life balance">work-life balance</a> and social consciousness.
</p><p>According to authors from <a href="/wiki/Florida_International_University" title="Florida International University">Florida International University</a>, original research performed by Howe and Strauss as well as Yu &amp; Miller suggest Baby Boomers resonate primarily with loyalty, work ethic, steady career path, and compensation when it comes to their professional lives.<sup id="cite_ref-FIU_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIU-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Generation X on the other hand, started shifting preferences towards an improved work-life balance with a heightened focus on individual advancement, stability, and job satisfaction.<sup id="cite_ref-FIU_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIU-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, millennials place an emphasis on producing meaningful work, finding a creative outlet, and have a preference for immediate feedback.<sup id="cite_ref-FIU_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIU-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In the article "Challenges of the Work of the Future," it is also stressed that millennials working at the knowledge-based jobs very often assume personal responsibility in order to make the most of what they do. As they are not satisfied with remaining for a long period of time at the same job, their career paths become more dynamic and less predictable.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Findings also suggest the introduction of social media has augmented collaborative skills and created a preference for a team-oriented environment.<sup id="cite_ref-FIU_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIU-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In the 2010 the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Business_and_Psychology" title="Journal of Business and Psychology">Journal of Business and Psychology</a>,</i> contributors Myers and Sadaghiani find millennials "expect close relationships and frequent feedback from supervisors" to be a main point of differentiation.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_and_Sadaghiani_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers_and_Sadaghiani-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Multiple studies observe millennials’ associating job satisfaction with free flow of information, strong connectivity to supervisors, and more immediate feedback.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_and_Sadaghiani_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers_and_Sadaghiani-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Hershatter and Epstein, researchers from <a href="/wiki/Emory_University" title="Emory University">Emory University</a>, argue a lot of these traits can be linked to millennials entering the educational system on the cusp of academic reform, which created a much more structured educational system.<sup id="cite_ref-Hershatter_and_Epstein_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hershatter_and_Epstein-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Some argue in the wake of these reforms, such as the <a href="/wiki/No_Child_Left_Behind_Act" title="No Child Left Behind Act">No Child Left Behind Act</a>, millennials have increasingly sought the aid of mentors and advisers, leading to 66% of millennials seeking a flat work environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Hershatter_and_Epstein_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hershatter_and_Epstein-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hershatter and Epstein also stress a growing importance on work-life balance. Studies show nearly one-third of students' top priority is to "balance personal and professional life".<sup id="cite_ref-Hershatter_and_Epstein_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hershatter_and_Epstein-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The Brain Drain Study shows nearly 9 out of 10 millennials place an importance on work-life balance, with additional surveys demonstrating the generation to favor familial over corporate values.<sup id="cite_ref-Hershatter_and_Epstein_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hershatter_and_Epstein-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Studies also show a preference for work-life balance, which contrasts to the Baby Boomers' work-centric attitude.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_and_Sadaghiani_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers_and_Sadaghiani-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Data also suggests millennials are driving a shift towards the public service sector. In 2010, Myers and Sadaghiani published research in the <i>Journal of Business and Psychology</i> stating heightened participation in the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a> and <a href="/wiki/AmeriCorps" title="AmeriCorps">AmeriCorps</a> as a result of millennials, with volunteering being at all-time highs.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_and_Sadaghiani_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers_and_Sadaghiani-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Volunteer activity between 2007 and 2008 show the Millennial age group experienced almost three-times the increase of the overall population, which is consistent with a survey of 130 college upperclassmen depicting an emphasis on altruism in their upbringing.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_and_Sadaghiani_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers_and_Sadaghiani-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> This has led, according to a Harvard University Institute of Politics, six out of ten millennials to consider a career in public service.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_and_Sadaghiani_75-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers_and_Sadaghiani-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2014 Brookings publication shows a generational adherence to corporate social responsibility, with the National Society of High School Scholars (NSHSS) 2013 survey and Universum’s 2011 survey, depicting a preference to work for companies engaged in the betterment of society.<sup id="cite_ref-Brookings_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Millennials' shift in attitudes has led to data depicting 64% of millennials would take a 60% pay cut to pursue a career path aligned with their passions, and financial institutions have fallen out of favor with banks comprising 40% of the generation's least liked brands.<sup id="cite_ref-Brookings_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, author Ron Alsop called the millennials "Trophy Kids,"<sup id="cite_ref-Alsop08_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alsop08-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> a term that reflects a trend in competitive sports, as well as many other aspects of life, where mere participation is frequently enough for a reward. It has been reported that this is an issue in corporate environments.<sup id="cite_ref-Alsop08_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alsop08-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Some employers are concerned that millennials have too great expectations from the workplace.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_Trophy_Kids_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_Trophy_Kids-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Some studies predict they will switch jobs frequently, holding many more jobs than Gen Xers due to their great expectations.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Psychologist Jean Twenge reports data suggests there are differences between older and younger millennials regarding workplace expectations, with younger millennials being "more practical" and "more attracted to industries with steady work and are more likely to say they are willing to work overtime" which Twenge attributes to younger millennials coming of age following the financial crisis of 2007-2008.<sup id="cite_ref-NYMAG_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYMAG-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is also a contention that the major differences are found solely between millennials and Generation X. Researchers from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri" title="University of Missouri">University of Missouri</a> and The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tennessee" title="University of Tennessee">University of Tennessee</a> conducted a study based on measurement equivalence to determine if such a difference does in fact exist.<sup id="cite_ref-MWEP_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MWEP-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The study looked at 1,860 participants who had completed the Multidimensional Work Ethic Profile (MWEP), a survey aimed at measuring identification with work-ethic characteristics, across a 12-year period spanning from 1996 to 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-MWEP_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MWEP-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The results of the findings suggest the main difference in work ethic sentiments arose between the two most recent generational cohorts, Generation X and millennials, with relatively small variances between the two generations and their predecessor, the Baby Boomers.<sup id="cite_ref-MWEP_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MWEP-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>That said, some research fails to find convincing differences. A meta study conducted by researchers from The <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> and The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Army" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a> Research Institute for the Behavioral and Social Sciences questions the validity of workplace differences across any generational cohort. According to the researchers, disagreement in which events to include when assigning generational cohorts, as well as varied opinions on which age ranges to include in each generational category are the main drivers behind their skepticism.<sup id="cite_ref-Workplace_Meta_Study_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Workplace_Meta_Study-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The analysis of 20 research reports focusing on the three work-related factors of job satisfaction, organizational commitment and intent to turn over proved any variation was too small to discount the impact of employee tenure and aging of individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Workplace_Meta_Study_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Workplace_Meta_Study-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Newer research shows that millennials change jobs for the same reasons as other generations—namely, more money and a more innovative work environment. They look for versatility and flexibility in the workplace, and strive for a strong work–life balance in their jobs<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and have similar career aspirations to other generations, valuing financial security and a diverse workplace just as much as their older colleagues.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_views">Political views</span></h2>
<p>Surveys of political attitudes among millennials in the United Kingdom have suggested increasingly <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">social liberal</a> views, as well as higher overall support for <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberal</a> economic policies than preceding generations. They are more likely to support <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a> and the legalization of drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-economist.com_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i> parallels this with millennials in the United States, whose attitudes are more supportive of social liberal policies and same-sex marriage relative to other demographics.<sup id="cite_ref-economist.com_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> They are also more likely to oppose <a href="/wiki/Animal_testing" title="Animal testing">animal testing</a> for medical purposes than older generations.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Research">Pew Research</a> described Millennials as "the force of the youth vote" and as part of the  political conversation which helped elect the <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">first U.S. black president</a>, describing Millennials as between 12 and 27 during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">2008 U.S Presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2018_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2018-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>   
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Bernie Sanders</a>, a self-proclaimed <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic socialist">democratic socialist</a> and democratic candidate in the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_States_presidential_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2016 United States presidential election">2016 United States presidential election</a>, was the most popular candidate among Millennial voters in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_primary" title="United States presidential primary">primary phase</a>, having garnered more votes from people under 30 in 21 states than the major parties' candidates, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>, did combined.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In April 2016, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> viewed him as changing the way millennials viewed politics, saying, "He's not moving a party to the left. He's moving a generation to the left."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Bernie Sanders referred to millennials as "the least prejudiced generation in the history of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> A 2014 poll for the <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_in_the_United_States" title="Libertarianism in the United States">libertarian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Reason_(magazine)" title="Reason (magazine)">Reason</a></i> magazine suggested that American millennials were <a href="/wiki/Social_liberal" class="mw-redirect" title="Social liberal">social liberals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_centrism" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiscal centrism">fiscal centrists</a>, more often than their global peers. The magazine predicted that millennials would become more <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a> on fiscal issues once they started paying taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, the majority of millennials opposed <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">the British withdrawal from the European Union</a>. Blaming <a href="/wiki/Baby_boomers" title="Baby boomers">Baby boomers</a>, who largely supported the referendum, one commenter said: "The younger generation has lost the right to live and work in 27 other countries. We will never know the full extent of the lost opportunities, friendships, marriages and experiences we will be denied."<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> phrased this as "we let you steal our future", reporting high voter turnout among those over 55 years of age and low voter turnout among those under 34 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitudes">Attitudes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg/220px-Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg/330px-Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg/440px-Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Safe_Space_America_Truck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Safe Space America vehicle in 2016, which is used to promote the availability and creation of <a href="/wiki/Safe_space" title="Safe space">safe spaces</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Neil_Howe" title="Neil Howe">Neil Howe</a> believes that a defining trait of millennials is that they are more likely to support <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">political correctness</a> than members of older generations.<sup id="cite_ref-HowePC_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HowePC-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, a <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Research">Pew Research</a> study found 40% of millennials in the United States supported government restriction of public speech offensive to minority groups. Support for restricting offensive speech was lower among older generations, with 27% of Gen Xers, 24% of Baby Boomers, and only 12% of the Silent Generation supporting such restrictions. Pew Research noted similar age related trends in the United Kingdom, but not in Germany and Spain, where millennials were less supportive of restricting offensive speech than older generations. In France, Italy and Poland no significant age differences were observed.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In the U.S. and UK, millennials have brought changes to higher education via drawing attention to <a href="/wiki/Microaggressions" class="mw-redirect" title="Microaggressions">microaggressions</a> and advocating for implementation of <a href="/wiki/Safe_space" title="Safe space">safe spaces</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trigger_warnings" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigger warnings">trigger warnings</a> in the university setting. Critics of such changes have raised concerns regarding their impact on <a href="/wiki/Free_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Free speech">free speech</a>, asserting these changes can promote <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a>, while proponents have described these changes as promoting inclusiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-HowePC_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HowePC-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics_in_the_United_States">Demographics in the United States</span></h2>
<p>Millennial population size varies, depending on the definition used. <a href="/wiki/William_Strauss_and_Neil_Howe" class="mw-redirect" title="William Strauss and Neil Howe">William Strauss and Neil Howe</a> projected in their 1991 book <i>Generations</i> that the U.S. Millennial population would be 76 million.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Later,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (December 2014)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> using dates ranging from 1982 to 2004, Neil Howe revised the number to over 95 million people (in the U.S.).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In a 2012 <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i> magazine</a> article, it was estimated that there were approximately 80 million U.S. millennials.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a>, using birth dates ranging from 1982 to 2000, stated the estimated number of U.S. millennials in 2015 was 83.1 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> found that millennials surpassed Baby Boomers to become the largest living generation in the United States. By analyzing 2015 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">U.S Census</a> data they found there were 75.4 million millennials, based on Pew's definition of the generation which ranges from 1981 to 1997, compared to 74.9 million Baby Boomers.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>However with their revised end date of 1996, millennials are expected to surpass boomers in size in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_prospects">Economic prospects</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG/220px-1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG/330px-1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG/440px-1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3488" data-file-height="2616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1_-_Hamburg_1._Mai_2014_07.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German young adults protest <a href="/wiki/Youth" title="Youth">youth</a> unemployment at a 2014 event</div></div></div>
<p>Economic prospects for some millennials have declined largely due to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> in the late 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto4_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto5_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Several governments have instituted major youth employment schemes out of fear of social unrest due to the dramatically increased rates of <a href="/wiki/Youth_unemployment" title="Youth unemployment">youth unemployment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lostgeny_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lostgeny-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, youth unemployment levels were very high (56% in Spain,<sup id="cite_ref-auto6_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> 44% in Italy,<sup id="cite_ref-auto7_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> 35% in the Baltic states, 19.1% in Britain<sup id="cite_ref-auto8_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto8-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and more than 20% in many more countries). In 2009, leading commentators began to worry about the long-term social and economic effects of the unemployment.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Unemployment levels in other areas of the world were also high, with the youth <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_rate_in_the_U.S." class="mw-redirect" title="Unemployment rate in the U.S.">unemployment rate in the U.S.</a> reaching a record 19.1% in July 2010 since the statistic started being gathered in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, unemployment among youths in July 2009 was 15.9%, the highest it had been in 11 years.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Underemployment" title="Underemployment">Underemployment</a> is also a major factor.
In the U.S. the economic difficulties have led to dramatic increases in youth poverty, unemployment, and the numbers of young people living with their parents.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In April 2012, it was reported that half of all new college graduates in the US were still either unemployed or underemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> It has been argued that this unemployment rate and poor economic situation has given millennials a rallying call with the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Occupy_Wall_Street" title="Occupy Wall Street">Occupy Wall Street</a> movement.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> However, according to Christine Kelly, Occupy is not a <a href="/wiki/Youth_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth movement">youth movement</a> and has participants that vary from the very young to very old.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A variety of names have emerged in various European countries hard hit following the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">financial crisis of 2007–2008</a> to designate young people with limited employment and career prospects.<sup id="cite_ref-Itano_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Itano-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> These groups can be considered to be more or less synonymous with millennials, or at least major sub-groups in those countries. <i>The Generation of €700</i> is a term popularized by the <a href="/wiki/Media_in_Greece#Mass_media" class="mw-redirect" title="Media in Greece">Greek mass media</a> and refers to educated <a href="/wiki/Greek_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek people">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Twixter" title="Twixter">twixters</a> of urban centers who generally fail to establish a <a href="/wiki/Career" title="Career">career</a>. In Greece, young adults are being "excluded from the labor market" and some "leave their country of origin to look for better options". They're being "marginalized and face uncertain working conditions" in jobs that are unrelated to their educational background, and receive the minimum allowable base <a href="/wiki/Salary" title="Salary">salary</a> of €700 per month. This generation evolved in circumstances leading to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">Greek debt crisis</a> and some participated in the <a href="/wiki/Anti-austerity_movement_in_Greece" title="Anti-austerity movement in Greece">2010–2011 Greek protests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, they're referred to as the <i>mileurista</i> (for €1,000 per month),<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> in France "The Precarious Generation,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>" and as in Spain, Italy also has the "milleurista"; generation of 1,000 euros (per month).<sup id="cite_ref-Itano_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Itano-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, millennials in New York City were reported as earning 20% less than the generation before them, as a result of entering the workforce during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession_in_the_United_States" title="Great Recession in the United States">great recession</a>. Despite higher college attendance rates than Generation X, many were stuck in low-paid jobs, with the percentage of degree-educated young adults working in low-wage industries rising from 23% to 33% between 2000 and 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, research from the <a href="/wiki/Resolution_Foundation" title="Resolution Foundation">Resolution Foundation</a> found millennials in the UK earned £8,000 less in their 20s than Generation X, describing millennials as "on course to become the first generation to earn less than the one before".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Generation Flux</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">neologism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychographic">psychographic</a> (not demographic) designation coined by <i><a href="/wiki/Fast_Company_(magazine)" title="Fast Company (magazine)">Fast Company</a></i> for <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_States" title="Economy of the United States">American employees</a> who need to make several changes in <a href="/wiki/Career" title="Career">career</a> throughout their working lives due to the chaotic nature of the job market following the Great Recession. Societal change has been <a href="/wiki/Unbundling" title="Unbundling">accelerated</a> by the use of <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_computing" title="Mobile computing">mobile computing</a>, and other new technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-Robert_Safian_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_Safian-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Those in "Generation Flux" have birth-years in the ranges of both <a href="/wiki/Generation_X" title="Generation X">Generation X</a> and millennials. "Generation Sell" was used by author <a href="/wiki/William_Deresiewicz" title="William Deresiewicz">William Deresiewicz</a> to describe millennials' interest in small businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-Generation_Sell_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Generation_Sell-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Millennials are expected to make up approximately half of the U.S. workforce by 2020. Millennials are the most highly educated and culturally diverse group of all generations, and have been regarded as hard to please when it comes to employers.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> To address these new challenges, many large firms are currently studying the social and behavioral patterns of millennials and are trying to devise programs that decrease intergenerational estrangement, and increase relationships of reciprocal understanding between older employees and millennials. The UK's <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Leadership_%26_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of Leadership &amp; Management">Institute of Leadership &amp; Management</a> researched the gap in understanding between Millennial recruits and their managers in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Ashridge_Business_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Ashridge Business School">Ashridge Business School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The findings included high expectations for advancement, salary and for a coaching relationship with their manager, and suggested that organizations will need to adapt to accommodate and make the best use of millennials. In an example of a company trying to do just this, <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> conducted training programs that used actors to portray millennials who assertively sought more <a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">feedback</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moral_responsibility" title="Moral responsibility">responsibility</a>, and involvement in decision making. After the performance, employees discussed and debated the generational differences they saw played out.<sup id="cite_ref-Alsop08_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alsop08-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Millennials have benefited the least from the economic recovery following the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a>, as average incomes for this generation have fallen at twice the general adult population's total drop and are likely to be on a path toward lower incomes for at least another decade. A <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg L.P.</a> article wrote that "Three and a half years after the worst recession since the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, the earnings and employment gap between those in the under-35 population and their parents and grandparents threatens to unravel the American dream of each generation doing better than the last. The nation's younger workers have benefited least from an economic recovery that has been the most uneven in recent history."<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanDream_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanDream-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, millennials were entering an increasingly multi-generational workplace.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Even though research has shown that millennials are joining the workforce during a tough economic time they still have remained optimistic, as shown when about nine out of ten millennials surveyed by the Pew Research Center said that they currently have enough money or that they will eventually reach their long-term financial goals.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Peter_Pan_generation">Peter Pan generation</span></h2>
<p>American sociologist Kathleen Shaputis labeled millennials as the <i><a href="/wiki/Boomerang_Generation" title="Boomerang Generation">Boomerang Generation</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan" title="Peter Pan">Peter Pan</a> generation</i>, because of the members' perceived tendency for delaying some <a href="/wiki/Rites_of_passage" class="mw-redirect" title="Rites of passage">rites of passage</a> into adulthood for longer periods than most generations before them. These labels were also a reference to a trend toward members living with their parents for longer periods than previous generations.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaputis_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaputis-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Kimberly Palmer regards the high cost of housing and higher education, and the relative affluence of older generations, as among the factors driving the trend.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Questions regarding a clear definition of what it means to be an adult also impacts a debate about delayed transitions into adulthood and the emergence of a new life stage, Emerging Adulthood. A 2012 study by professors at <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young_University" title="Brigham Young University">Brigham Young University</a> found that college students were more likely to define "adult" based on certain personal abilities and characteristics rather than more traditional "rite of passage" events.<sup id="cite_ref-Nelson07_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson07-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Larry Nelson noted that "In prior generations, you get married and you start a career and you do that immediately. What young people today are seeing is that approach has led to divorces, to people unhappy with their careers … The majority want to get married […] they just want to do it right the first time, the same thing with their careers."<sup id="cite_ref-Nelson07_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson07-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Their expectations have had a dampening effect on millennials' rate of marriage. 
</p><p>A 2013 joint study by sociologists at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> found that the decline and disappearance of stable full-time jobs with <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance_in_the_United_States" title="Health insurance in the United States">health insurance</a> and pensions for people who lack a college degree has had profound effects on <a href="/wiki/Working-class_Americans" class="mw-redirect" title="Working-class Americans">working-class Americans</a>, who now are less likely to marry and have children within marriage than those with college degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Data from a 2014 study of U.S. millennials revealed over 56% of this cohort considers themselves as part of the working class, with only approximately 35% considering themselves as part of the middle class; this class identity is the lowest polling of any generation.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research by the <a href="/wiki/Urban_Institute" title="Urban Institute">Urban Institute</a> conducted in 2014, projected that if current trends continue, millennials will have a lower marriage rate compared to previous generations, predicting that by age 40, 30.7% of millennial women will remain single, approximately twice the share of their single Gen X counterparts. The data showed similar trends for males.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> A 2016 study from <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Research">Pew Research</a> showed millennials delay some activities considered rites of passage of adulthood with data showing young adults aged 18–34 were more likely to live with parents than with a relationship partner, an unprecedented occurrence since data collection began in 1880. Data also showed a significant increase in the percentage of young adults living with parents compared to the previous demographic cohort, <a href="/wiki/Generation_X" title="Generation X">Generation X</a>, with 23% of young adults aged 18–34 living with parents in 2000, rising to 32% in 2014. Additionally, in 2000, 43% of those aged 18–34 were married or living with a partner, with this figure dropping to 31.6% in 2014. High student debt is described as one reason for continuing to live with parents, but may not be the dominant factor for this shift as the data shows the trend is stronger for those without a college education. Richard Fry, a senior economist for Pew Research said of millennials, "they're the group much more likely to live with their parents." furthering "they're concentrating more on school, careers and work and less focused on forming new families, spouses or partners and children".<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a cross-generational study comparing millennials to Generation X conducted at <a href="/wiki/Wharton_School_of_Business" class="mw-redirect" title="Wharton School of Business">Wharton School of Business</a>, more than half of Millennial undergraduates surveyed do not plan to have children. The researchers compared surveys of the Wharton graduating class of 1992 and 2012. In 1992, 78% of women planned to eventually have children dropping to 42% in 2012. The results were similar for male students. The research revealed among both genders the proportion of undergraduates who reported they eventually planned to have children had dropped in half over the course of a generation.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h2>
<p>In the U.S., millennials are the least likely to be religious when compared to older generations.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> There is a trend towards <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">irreligion</a> that has been increasing since the 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> 29 percent of Americans born between 1983 and 1994 are irreligious, as opposed to 21 percent born between 1963 and 1981, 15 percent born between 1948 and 1962 and only 7 percent born before 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> A 2005 study looked at 1,385 people aged 18 to 25 and found that more than half of those in the study said that they pray regularly before a meal. One-third said that they discussed <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_United_States" title="Religion in the United States">religion</a> with friends, attended religious services, and read religious material weekly. Twenty-three percent of those studied did not identify themselves as religious practitioners.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> A Pew Research Center study on millennials shows that of those between 18–29 years old, only 3% of these emerging adults self-identified as "<a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheists</a>" and only 4% self-identified as "<a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnostics</a>". Overall, 25% of millennials are "Nones" and 75% are religiously affiliated.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Over half of millennials polled in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> in 2013 said they had "no religion nor attended a place of worship", other than for a wedding or a funeral. 25% said they "believe in a <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>", while 19% believed in a "spiritual greater power" and 38% said they did not believe in God nor any other "greater spiritual power". The poll also found 41% thought <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Religion in the United Kingdom">religion</a> was "the cause of evil" in the world more often than good.<sup id="cite_ref-YouGov_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YouGov-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/British_Social_Attitudes_Survey" title="British Social Attitudes Survey">British Social Attitudes Survey</a> found that 71% of British 18-24 year-olds were not religious, with just 3% affiliated to the once-dominant <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_technology">Digital technology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg/220px-People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg" width="220" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg/330px-People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg/440px-People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2473" data-file-height="2845" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:People_using_cellphones_on_a_street_in_New_York.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three people who appear to be millennials using cellphones</div></div></div>
<p>In their 2007 book, authors Junco and Mastrodicasa expanded on the work of <a href="/wiki/William_Strauss" title="William Strauss">William Strauss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neil_Howe" title="Neil Howe">Neil Howe</a> to include research-based information about the personality profiles of millennials, especially as it relates to higher education. They conducted a large-sample (7,705) research study of <a href="/wiki/College" title="College">college</a> <a href="/wiki/Student" title="Student">students</a>. They found that Next Generation college students, born between 1983–1992, were frequently in touch with their parents and they used <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> at higher rates than people from <a href="/wiki/List_of_Generations" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Generations">other generations</a>. In their survey, they found that 97% of these students owned a <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a>, 94% owned a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phone</a>, and 56% owned an <a href="/wiki/MP3_player" title="MP3 player">MP3 player</a>. They also found that students spoke with their parents an average of 1.5 times a day about a wide range of topics. Other findings in the Junco and Mastrodicasa survey revealed 76% of students used <a href="/wiki/Instant_messaging" title="Instant messaging">instant messaging</a>, 92% of those reported <a href="/wiki/Human_multitasking" title="Human multitasking">multitasking</a> while instant messaging, 40% of them used <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a> to get most of their news, and 34% of students surveyed used the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> as their primary news source.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>  Older millennials came of age prior to widespread usage and availability of <a href="/wiki/Smartphones" class="mw-redirect" title="Smartphones">smartphones</a>, defined as those born 1988 and earlier, in contrast to younger millennials, those born in 1989 and later, who were exposed to this technology in their teen years.<sup id="cite_ref-NYMAG_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYMAG-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Gen Xers and millennials were the first to grow up with computers in their homes. In a 1999 speech at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Institute_of_Technology" title="New York Institute of Technology">New York Institute of Technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> Chairman and CEO <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a> encouraged America's teachers to use technology to serve the needs of the first generation of kids to grow up with the Internet.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Some millennials enjoy having hundreds of channels from <a href="/wiki/Cable_TV" class="mw-redirect" title="Cable TV">cable TV</a>. However, some other millennials do not even have a <a href="/wiki/TV" class="mw-redirect" title="TV">TV</a>, so they watch media over the Internet using smartphones and tablets.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> One of the most popular forms of media use by millennials is social networking.
