In 2010, research was published in the Elon Journal of Undergraduate Research which claimed that students who used social media and decided to quit showed the same withdrawal symptoms of a drug addict who quit their stimulant.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marc_Prensky" title="Marc Prensky">Marc Prensky</a> coined the term "<a href="/wiki/Digital_native" title="Digital native">digital native</a>" to describe "K through college" students in 2001, explaining they "represent the first generations to grow up with this new technology."<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Millennials are identified as "digital natives" by the Pew Research Center which conducted a survey titled <i>Millennials in Adulthood.</i><sup id="cite_ref-Millennials_in_Adulthood_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millennials_in_Adulthood-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Millennials use social networking sites, such as <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>, to create a different sense of belonging, make acquaintances, and to remain connected with friends.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Frontline_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Frontline (U.S. TV series)">Frontline</a> episode "Generation Like" there is discussion about millennials, their dependence on technology, and the ways the social media sphere is <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commoditized</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_identity">Cultural identity</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg/220px-Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg/330px-Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg/440px-Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2118" data-file-height="1431" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lesbian_Wedding_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two women celebrate a <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a> in 2007</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Strauss_%26_Howe" class="mw-redirect" title="Strauss &amp; Howe">Strauss &amp; Howe</a>'s book titled <i>Millennials Rising: The Next Great Generation</i> describes the Millennial generation as "civic-minded", rejecting the attitudes of the <a href="/wiki/Baby_Boomers" class="mw-redirect" title="Baby Boomers">Baby Boomers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Generation_X" title="Generation X">Generation X</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Since the 2000 U.S. Census, which allowed people to select more than one racial group, millennials in abundance have asserted the ideal that all their heritages should be respected, counted, and acknowledged.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Millennials are the children of Baby Boomers or Generation X, while some older members may have parents from the <a href="/wiki/Silent_Generation" title="Silent Generation">Silent Generation</a>. A 2013 poll in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> found that Generation Y was more "open-minded than their parents on controversial topics".<sup id="cite_ref-YouGov_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YouGov-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Of those surveyed, nearly 75% supported <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.
</p><p>A 2013 Pew Research Poll found that 84% of millennials, born since 1980, who were at that time between the ages of 18 and 32, favored <a href="/wiki/Legalization_of_marijuana" class="mw-redirect" title="Legalization of marijuana">legalizing the use of marijuana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto9_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the Pew Research Center also conducted research regarding generational identity.<sup id="cite_ref-pporg_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pporg-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> It was discovered that millennials are less likely to strongly identify with the generational term when compared to Generation X or to the Baby Boomers, with only 40% of those born between 1981 and 1997 identifying as part of the Millennial Generation. Among older millennials, those born 1981–1988, Pew Research found 43% personally identified as members of the older demographic cohort, Generation X, while only 35% identified as millennials. Among younger millennials (born 1989–1997), generational identity was not much stronger, with only 45% personally identifying as millennials. It was also found that millennials chose most often to define itself with more negative terms such as self-absorbed, wasteful or greedy. In this 2015 report, Pew defined millennials with birth years ranging from 1981 onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-pporg_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pporg-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Millennials came of age in a time where the entertainment industry began to be affected by the Internet.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-All_Songs_Considered_:_NPR_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-All_Songs_Considered_:_NPR-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In addition to millennials being the most ethnically and racially diverse compared to the generations older than they are, they are also on pace to be the most formally educated. As of  2008<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Millennials&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 39.6% of millennials between the ages of 18 and 24 were enrolled in college, which was an American record. Along with being educated, millennials also tend to upbeat. As stated above in the economic prospects section, about 9 out of 10 millennials feel as though they have enough money or that they will reach their long-term financial goals, even during the tough economic times, and they are more optimistic about the future of the U.S. Additionally, millennials are also more open to change than older generations. According to the Pew Research Center that did a survey in 2008, millennials are the most likely of any generation to self-identify as <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">liberals</a> and are also more supportive of <a href="/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States" title="Progressivism in the United States">progressive</a> domestic social agenda than older generations. Finally, millennials are less overtly religious than the older generations. About one in four millennials are unaffiliated with any religion, a considerably higher ratio than that of older generations when they were the ages of millennials.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
